"<jats:p>Earliest Paleocene <jats:italic>Purgatorius</jats:italic> often is regarded as the geologically oldest primate, but it has been known only from fossilized dentitions since it was first described half a century ago. The dentition of <jats:italic>Purgatorius</jats:italic> is more primitive than those of all known living and fossil primates, leading some researchers to suggest that it lies near the ancestry of all other primates; however, others have questioned its affinities to primates or even to placental mammals. Here we report the first (to our knowledge) nondental remains (tarsal bones) attributed to <jats:italic>Purgatorius</jats:italic> from the same earliest Paleocene deposits that have yielded numerous fossil dentitions of this poorly known mammal. Three independent phylogenetic analyses that incorporate new data from these fossils support primate affinities of <jats:italic>Purgatorius</jats:italic> among euarchontan mammals (primates, treeshrews, and colugos). Astragali and calcanei attributed to <jats:italic>Purgatorius</jats:italic> indicate a mobile ankle typical of arboreal euarchontan mammals generally and of Paleocene and Eocene plesiadapiforms specifically and provide the earliest fossil evidence of arboreality in primates and other euarchontan mammals. Postcranial specializations for arboreality in the earliest primates likely played a key role in the evolutionary success of this mammalian radiation in the Paleocene.</jats:p>", "DOI": "10.1073/pnas.1421707112", "type": "journal-article", "created": {"date-parts": [[2015, 1, 21]], "date-time": "2015-01-21T06:19:22Z", "timestamp": 1421821162000}, "page": "1487-1492", "update-policy": "http://dx.doi.org/10.1073/pnas.cm10313", "source": "Crossref", "is-referenced-by-count": 36, "title": ["Oldest known euarchontan tarsals and affinities of Paleocene Purgatorius to Primates"], "prefix": "10.1073", "volume": "112", "author": [{"given": "Stephen G.
